                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {

                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2000 408:6814"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="BriefCommunication"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2000 Macmillan Magazines Ltd."/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana is an important model system for identifying genes and determining their functions. Here we report the analysis of the genomic sequence of Arabidopsis. The sequenced regions cover 115.4&#8201;megabases of the 125-megabase genome and extend into centromeric regions. The evolution of Arabidopsis involved a whole-genome duplication, followed by subsequent gene loss and extensive local gene duplications, giving rise to a dynamic genome enriched by lateral gene transfer from a cyanobacterial-like ancestor of the plastid. The genome contains 25,498 genes encoding proteins from 11,000 families, similar to the functional diversity of Drosophila  and Caenorhabditis elegans&#8212; the other sequenced multicellular eukaryotes. Arabidopsis has many families of new proteins but also lacks several common protein families, indicating that the sets of common proteins have undergone differential expansion and contraction in the three multicellular eukaryotes. This is the first complete genome sequence of a plant and provides the foundations for more comprehensive comparison of conserved processes in all eukaryotes, identifying a wide range of plant-specific gene functions and establishing rapid systematic ways to identify genes for crop improvement."/>

                    <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i></h1>
                    <ul class="c-author-list js-etal-collapsed" data-etal="25" data-etal-small="3" data-test="authors-list" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"><li class="c-author-list__item"><a data-test="author-name" data-author-popup="group-1" href="#group-1">The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative</a></li></ul>

                    

                    <p class="c-article-info-details" data-container-section="info">
                        
    <a data-test="journal-link" href="/nature"><i data-test="journal-title">Nature</i></a>

                        <b data-test="journal-volume"><span class="u-visually-hidden">volume</span> 408</b>, <span class="u-visually-hidden">pages</span><span itemprop="pageStart">796</span>–<span itemprop="pageEnd">815</span>(<span data-test="article-publication-year">2000</span>)<a href="#citeas" class="c-article-info-details__cite-as u-hide-print" data-track="click" data-track-action="cite this article" data-track-label="link">Cite this article</a>
                    </p>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The flowering plant <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> is an important model system for identifying genes and determining their functions. Here we report the analysis of the genomic sequence of <i>Arabidopsis</i>. The sequenced regions cover 115.4 megabases of the 125-megabase genome and extend into centromeric regions. The evolution of <i>Arabidopsis</i> involved a whole-genome duplication, followed by subsequent gene loss and extensive local gene duplications, giving rise to a dynamic genome enriched by lateral gene transfer from a cyanobacterial-like ancestor of the plastid. The genome contains 25,498 genes encoding proteins from 11,000 families, similar to the functional diversity of <i>Drosophila </i> and <i>Caenorhabditis elegans—</i> the other sequenced multicellular eukaryotes. <i>Arabidopsis</i> has many families of new proteins but also lacks several common protein families, indicating that the sets of common proteins have undergone differential expansion and contraction in the three multicellular eukaryotes. This is the first complete genome sequence of a plant and provides the foundations for more comprehensive comparison of conserved processes in all eukaryotes, identifying a wide range of plant-specific gene functions and establishing rapid systematic ways to identify genes for crop improvement.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The plant and animal kingdoms evolved independently from unicellular eukaryotes and represent highly contrasting life forms. The genome sequences of <i>C. elegans</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The C. elegans Sequencing Consortium. Sequence and analysis of the genome of C. elegans. Science  282, 2012–2018 ( 1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1353">1</a></sup> and <i>Drosophila</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Adams, M. D. The genome sequence of Drosophila melanogaster. Science  287, 2185–2195 ( 2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1359">2</a></sup> reveal that metazoans share a great deal of genetic information required for developmental and physiological processes, but these genome sequences represent a limited survey of multicellular organisms. Flowering plants have unique organizational and physiological properties in addition to ancestral features conserved between plants and animals. The genome sequence of a plant provides a means for understanding the genetic basis of differences between plants and other eukaryotes, and provides the foundation for detailed functional characterization of plant genes.</p><p><i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> has many advantages for genome analysis, including a short generation time, small size, large number of offspring, and a relatively small nuclear genome. These advantages promoted the growth of a scientific community that has investigated the biological processes of <i>Arabidopsis </i> and has characterized many genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Meinke, D. W., Cherry, J. M., Dean, C., Rounsley, S. D. &amp; Koornneef, M. Arabidopsis thaliana: a model plant for genome analysis. Science 282, 662– 665 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1371">3</a></sup>. To support these activities, an international collaboration (the Arabidopsis Genome Initiative, AGI) began sequencing the genome in 1996. The sequences of chromosomes 2 and 4 have been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lin, X. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 2 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 761– 768 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1375">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mayer, K. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromsome 4 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 769– 777 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1378">5</a></sup>, and the accompanying Letters describe the sequences of chromosomes 1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Theologis, A.  et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 1 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 816– 820 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1381">6</a>), 3 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference  7" title="Salanoubat, M.  et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 3 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 820– 822 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1384"> 7</a>) and 5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tabata, S. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 5 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 820– 822 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1388">8</a>).</p><p>Here we report analysis of the completed <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome sequence, including annotation of predicted genes and assignment of functional categories. We also describe chromosome dynamics and architecture, the distribution of transposable elements and other repeats, the extent of lateral gene transfer from organelles, and the comparison of the genome sequence and structure to that of other <i>Arabidopsis</i> accessions (distinctive lines maintained by single-seed descent) and plant species. This report is the summation of work by experts interested in many biological processes selected to illuminate plant-specific functions including defence, photomorphogenesis, gene regulation, development, metabolism, transport and DNA repair.</p><p>The identification of many new members of receptor families, cellular components for plant-specific functions, genes of bacterial origin whose functions are now integrated with typical eukaryotic components, independent evolution of several families of transcription factors, and suggestions of as yet uncharacterized metabolic pathways are a few more highlights of this work. The implications of these discoveries are not only relevant for plant biologists, but will also affect agricultural science, evolutionary biology, bioinformatics, combinatorial chemistry, functional and comparative genomics, and molecular medicine.</p><p>
                <b>The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative</b>
              </p><p>Three groups contributed to the work reported here. The Genome Sequencing groups, arranged here in order of sequence contribution, sequenced and annotated assigned chromosomal regions. The Genome Analysis group carried out the analyses described. The Contributing Authors interpreted the genome analyses, incorporating other data and analyses, with respect to selected biological topics.</p><p>

              </p><p>C. Somerville.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Overview of sequencing strategy"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Overview of sequencing strategy</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>We used large-insert bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC), phage (P1) and transformation-competent artificial chromosome (TAC) libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Choi, S. D., Creelman, R., Mullet, J. &amp; Wing, R. A. Construction and characterisation of a bacterial artificial chromosome library from Arabidopsis thaliana. Weeds World 2, 17– 20 (1995)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1605">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Mozo, T., Fischer, S., Shizuya, H. &amp; Altmann, T. Construction and characterization of the IGF Arabidopsis BAC library. Mol. Gen. Genet. 258, 562–570 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1608">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lui, Y. -G., Mitsukawa, N., Vazquez-Tello, A. &amp; Whittier, R. F. Generation of a high-quality P1 library of Arabidopsis suitable for chromosome walking. Plant J. 7, 351– 358 (1995)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1611">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Lui, Y. -G.  et al. Complementation of plant mutants with large genomic DNA fragments by a transformation-competent artificial chromosome vector accelerates positional cloning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 96, 6535 –6540 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1614">12</a></sup> as the primary substrates for sequencing. Early stages of genome sequencing used 79 cosmid clones. Physical maps of the genome of accession Columbia were assembled by restriction fragment ‘fingerprint’ analysis of BAC clones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Marra, M. et al. A map or sequence analysis of the Arabidopsis thaliana genome. Nature Genet. 22, 265– 270 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1618">13</a></sup>, by hybridization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Mozo, T. et al. A complete BAC-based physical map of the Arabidopsis thaliana  genome. Nature Genet. 22, 271– 275 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1622">14</a></sup> or polymerase chain reaction (PCR)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Sato, S. et al. Structural analysis of Arabidopsis thaliana chromosome 5. I. Sequence features of the 1. 6 Mb regions covered by twenty physically assigned P1 clones. DNA Res. 4, 215– 230 (1997)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1626">15</a></sup> of sequence-tagged sites and by hybridization and Southern blotting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Bent, E., Johnson, S. &amp; Bancroft, I. BAC representation of two low-copy regions of the genome of Arabidopsis thaliana. Plant J. 13, 849–855 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1630">16</a></sup>. The resulting maps were integrated (http://nucleus/cshl.org/arabmaps/) with the genetic map and provided a foundation for assembling sets of contigs into sequence-ready tiling paths. End sequence (http://www.tigr.org/tdb/at/abe/bac_end_search.html) of 47,788 BAC clones was used to extend contigs from BACS anchored by marker content and to integrate contigs.</p><p>Ten contigs representing the chromosome arms and centromeric heterochromatin were assembled from 1,569 BAC, TAC, cosmid and P1 clones (average insert size 100 kilobases (kb)). Twenty-two PCR products were amplified directly from genomic DNA and sequenced to link regions not covered by cloned DNA or to optimize the minimal tiling path. Telomere sequence was obtained from specific yeast artificial chromosome (YAC) and phage clones, and from inverse polymerase chain reaction (IPCR) products derived from genomic DNA. Clone fingerprints, together with BAC end sequences, were generally adequate for selection of clones for sequencing over most of the genome. In the centromeric regions, these physical mapping methods were supplemented with genetic mapping to identify contig positions and orientation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Copenhaver, G. P.  et al. Genetic definition and sequence analysis of Arabidopsis  centromeres. Science 286, 2468– 2474 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1637">17</a></sup>.</p><p>Selected clones were sequenced on both strands and assembled using standard techniques. Comparison of independently derived sequence of overlapping regions and independent reassembly sequenced clones revealed accuracy rates between 99.99 and 99.999%. Over half of the sequence differences were between genomic and BAC clone sequence. All available sequenced genetic markers were integrated into sequence assemblies to verify sequence contigs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lin, X. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 2 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 761– 768 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1644">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mayer, K. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromsome 4 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 769– 777 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1647">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Theologis, A.  et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 1 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 816– 820 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1650">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Salanoubat, M.  et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 3 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 820– 822 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1653">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tabata, S. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 5 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 820– 822 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1656">8</a></sup>. The total length of sequenced regions, which extend from either the telomeres or ribosomal DNA repeats to the 180-base-pair (bp) centromeric repeats, is 115,409,949 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Estimates of the unsequenced centromeric and rDNA repeat regions measure roughly 10 megabases (Mb), yielding a genome size of about 125 Mb, in the range of the 50–150 Mb haploid content estimated by different methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Meyerowitz, E. M. &amp; Somerville, C. R. Arabidopsis  (Cold Spring Harbor Laboratory Press, Cold Spring Harbor, New York, 1994)" href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1663">18</a></sup>. In general, features such as gene density, expression levels and repeat distribution are very consistent across the five chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), and these are described in detail in reports on individual chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lin, X. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 2 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 761– 768 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1670">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mayer, K. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromsome 4 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 769– 777 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1673">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Theologis, A.  et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 1 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 816– 820 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1676">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Salanoubat, M.  et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 3 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 820– 822 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1679">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tabata, S. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 5 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 820– 822 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1682">8</a></sup> and in the analysis of centromere, telomere and rDNA sequences.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary statistics of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/35048692/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Representation of the Arabidopsis chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Representation of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="600" height="641"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Each chromosome is represented as a coloured bar. Sequenced portions are red, telomeric and centromeric regions are light blue, heterochromatic knobs are shown black and the rDNA repeat regions are magenta. The unsequenced telomeres 2N and 4N are depicted with dashed lines. Telomeres are not drawn to scale. Images of DAPI-stained chromosomes were kindly supplied by P. Fransz. The frequency of features was given pseudo-colour assignments, from red (high density) to deep blue (low density). Gene density (‘Genes’) ranged from 38 per 100 kb to 1 gene per 100 kb; expressed sequence tag matches (‘ESTs’) ranged from more than 200 per 100 kb to 1 per 100 kb. Transposable element densities (‘TEs’) ranged from 33 per 100 kb to 1 per 100 kb. Mitochondrial and chloroplast insertions (‘MT/CP’) were assigned black and green tick marks, respectively. Transfer RNAs and small nucleolar RNAs (‘RNAs’) were assigned black and red ticks marks, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We used tRNAscan-SE 1.21 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: A program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 ( 1997)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1712">19</a>) and manual inspection to identify 589 cytoplasmic transfer RNAs, 27 organelle-derived tRNAs and 13 pseudogenes—more than in any other genome sequenced to date. All 46 tRNA families needed to decode all possible 61 codons were found, defining the completeness of the functional set. Several highly amplified families of tRNAs were found on the same strand<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Theologis, A.  et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 1 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 816– 820 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1716">6</a></sup>; excluding these, each amino acid is decoded by 10–41 tRNAs.</p><p>The spliceosomal RNAs (U1, U2, U4, U5, U6) have all been experimentally identified in <i>Arabidopsis</i>. The previously identified sequences for all RNAs were found in the genome, except for U5 where the most similar counterpart was 92% identical. Between 10 and 16 copies of each small nuclear RNA (snRNA) were found across all chromosomes, dispersed as singletons or in small groups. </p><p>The small nucleolar RNAs (snoRNAs) consist of two subfamilies, the C/D box snoRNAs, which includes 36 <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes, and the H/ACA box snoRNAs, for which no members have been identified in <i>Arabidopsis</i>. U3 is the most numerous of the C/D box snoRNAs, with eight copies found in the genome. We identified forty-five additional C/D box snoRNAs using software (www.rna.wustl.edu/snoRNAdb/) that detects snoRNAs that guide ribose methylation of ribosomal RNA.</p><p>A combination of algorithms, all optimized with parameters based on known <i> Arabidopsis</i> gene structures, was used to define gene structure. We used similarities to known protein and expressed sequence tag (EST) sequence to refine gene models. Eighty per cent of the gene structures predicted by the three centres involved were completely consistent, 93% of ESTs matched gene models, and less than 1% of ESTs matched predicted non-coding regions, indicating that most potential genes were identified. The sensitivity and selectivity of the gene prediction software used in this report has been comprehensively and independently assessed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Pavy, N. et al. Evaluation of gene prediction software using a genomic data set: application to Arabidopsis thaliana sequences. BioInformatics  15, 887–900 ( 1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1742">20</a></sup>.</p><p>The 25,498 genes predicted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Tab1">Table 1</a>) is the largest gene set published to date: <i>C. elegans</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The C. elegans Sequencing Consortium. Sequence and analysis of the genome of C. elegans. Science  282, 2012–2018 ( 1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1754">1</a></sup> has 19,099 genes and <i>Drosophila</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Adams, M. D. The genome sequence of Drosophila melanogaster. Science  287, 2185–2195 ( 2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1760">2</a></sup> 13,601 genes. <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i> C. elegans</i> have similar gene density, whereas <i>Drosophila</i> has a lower gene density; <i>Arabidopsis</i> also has a significantly greater extent of tandem gene duplications and segmental duplications, which may account for its larger gene set.</p><p>The rDNA repeat regions on chromosomes 2 and 4 were not sequenced because of their known repetitive structure and content. The centromeric regions are not completely sequenced owing to large blocks of monotonic repeats such as 5S rDNA and 180-bp repeats. The sequence continues to be extended further into centromeric and other regions of complex sequence.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Characterization of the coding regions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Characterization of the coding regions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>To assess the similarities and differences of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> gene complement compared with other sequenced eukaryotic genomes, we assigned functional categories to the complete set of <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes. For chromosome 4 genes and the yeast genome, predicted functions were previously manually assigned<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mayer, K. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromsome 4 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 769– 777 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1794">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Mewes, H. W.  et al. Overview of the yeast genome. Nature  387 (Suppl.) 7–65 (1997 )." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1797">21</a></sup>. All other predicted proteins were automatically assigned to these functional categories<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Frishman, D.  et al. Functional and structural genomics using PEDANT.  BioInformatics (in the press)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1801">22</a></sup>, assuming that conserved sequences reflect common functional relationships.</p><p>The functions of 69% of the genes were classified according to sequence similarity to proteins of known function in all organisms; only 9% of the genes have been characterized experimentally (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig2">Fig.&nbsp;2a </a>). Generally similar proportions of gene products were predicted to be targeted to the secretory pathway and mitochondria in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and yeast, and up to 14% of the gene products are likely to be targeted to the chloroplast (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The significant proportion of genes with predicted functions involved in metabolism, gene regulation and defence is consistent with previous analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mayer, K. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromsome 4 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 769– 777 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1817">5</a></sup>. Roughly 30% of the 25,498 predicted gene products, (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>), comprising both plant-specific proteins and proteins with similarity to genes of unknown function from other organisms, could not be assigned to functional categories. </p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Functional analysis of Arabidopsis genes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Functional analysis of <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="600" height="780"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Proportion of predicted <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes in different functional categories. <b>b</b>, Comparison of functional categories between organisms. Subsets of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> proteome containing all proteins that fall into a common functional class were assembled. Each subset was searched against the complete set of translations from <i>Escherichia coli</i>, <i> Synechocystis</i> sp. PCC6803, <i>Saccharomyces cerevisae</i>, <i>Drosophila </i>, <i>C. elegans</i> and a <i>Homo sapiens</i> non-redundant protein database. The percentage of <i>Arabidopsis</i> proteins in a particular subset that had a BLASTP match with <i>E</i> ≤ 10<sup>-30</sup> to the respective reference genome is shown. This reflects the measure of sequence conservation of proteins within this particular functional category between <i> Arabidopsis</i> and the respective reference genome. <i>y</i> axis, 0.1 = 10%.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To compare the functional catagories in more detail, we compared data from the complete genomes of <i>Escherichia coli</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Blattner, F. R.  et al. The complete genome sequence of Escherichia coli K-12. Science 277, 1453–1462 (1997)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1888">23</a></sup>, <i>Synechocystis </i> sp.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kotani, H. &amp; Tabata, S. Lessons from the sequencing of the genome of a unicellular cyanobacterium, Synechocystis SP. PCC6803. Annu. Rev. Plant Physiol. Plant Mol. Biol. 49, 151–171 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1895">24</a></sup>, <i>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Mewes, H. W.  et al. Overview of the yeast genome. Nature  387 (Suppl.) 7–65 (1997 )." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1901">21</a></sup>, <i> C. elegans</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The C. elegans Sequencing Consortium. Sequence and analysis of the genome of C. elegans. Science  282, 2012–2018 ( 1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1908">1</a></sup> and <i>Drosophila</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Adams, M. D. The genome sequence of Drosophila melanogaster. Science  287, 2185–2195 ( 2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d33224e1914">2</a></sup>, and a non-redundant protein set of <i>Homo sapiens</i>, with the <i>Arabidopsis </i> genome data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), using a stringent BLASTP threshold value of <i>E</i> &lt; 10<sup>-30</sup>. The proportion of <i> Arabidopsis</i> proteins having related counterparts in eukaryotic genomes varies by a factor of 2 to 3 depending on the functional category. Only 8–23% of <i>Arabidopsis</i> proteins involved in transcription have related genes in other eukaryotic genomes, reflecting the independent evolution of many plant transcription factors. In contrast, 48–60% of genes involved in protein synthesis have counterparts in the other eukaryotic genomes, reflecting highly conserved gene functions. The relatively high proportion of matches between <i>Arabidopsis</i> and bacterial proteins in the categories ‘metabolism’ and ‘energy’ reflects both the acquisition of bacterial genes from the ancestor of the plastid and high conservation of sequences across all species. Finally, a comparison between unicellular and multicellular eukaryotes indicates that <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes involved in cellular communication and signal transduction have more counterparts in multicellular eukaryotes than in yeast, reflecting the need for sets of genes for communication in multicellular organisms.</p><p>Pronounced redundancy in the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome is evident in segmental duplications and tandem arrays, and many other genes with high levels of sequence conservation are also scattered over the genome. Sequence similarity exceeding a BLASTP value <i>E</i> &lt; 10<sup>-20</sup> and extending over at least 80% of the protein length were used as parameters to identify protein families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Tab2">Table 2</a>). A total of 11,601 protein types were identified. Thirty-five per cent of the predicted proteins are unique in the genome, and the proportion of proteins belonging to families of more than five members is substantially higher in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (37.4%) than in <i>Drosophila</i> (12.1%) or <i>C.&nbsp;elegans</i> (24.0%). The absolute number of <i>Arabidopsis</i> gene families and singletons (types) is in the same range as the other multicellular eukaryotes, indicating that a proteome of 11,000–15,000 types is sufficient for a wide diversity of multicellular life. The proportion of gene families with more than two members is considerably more pronounced in <i>Arabidopsis</i> than in other eukaryotes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig3"> Fig. 3</a>). As segmental duplication is responsible for 6,303 gene duplications (see below), the extent of tandem gene duplications accounts for a significant proportion of the increased family size. These features of the <i>Arabidopsis </i>, and presumably other plant genomes, may indicate more relaxed constraints on genome size in plants, or a more prominent role of unequal crossing over to generate new gene copies.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Proportion of genes in different organisms present as either singletons or in paralogous families</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/35048692/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Distribution of tandemly repeated gene arrays in the Arabidopsis  genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Distribution of tandemly repeated gene arrays in the <i>Arabidopsis </i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="600" height="234"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Tandemly repeated gene arrays were identified using the BLASTP program with a threshold of <i>E</i> &lt; 10<sup>-20</sup>. One unrelated gene among cluster members was tolerated. The histogram gives the number of clusters in the genome containing 2 to <i>n</i> similar gene units in tandem.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Conserved protein domains revealed more informative differences through INTERPRO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Apweiler, R.  et al. INTERPRO (http://www. ebi. ac. uk/interpro/). Collaborative Computer Project 11 Newsletter no. 10 (Cambridge, 2000 )." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2014">25</a></sup> analysis of the predicted gene products from <i> Arabidopsis</i>, <i>S. cerevisiae</i>, <i>C. elegans</i> and <i>Drosophila </i>. Statistically over-represented domains, and those that are absent from the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome, indicate domains that may have been gained or lost during the evolution of plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information </a> Table 1). Proteins containing the Pro-Pro-Arg repeat, which is involved in RNA stabilization and RNA processing, are over-represented as compared to yeast, fly and worm; 400 proteins containing this signature were detected in <i>Arabidopsis</i> compared with only 10 in total in yeast, <i>Drosophila </i> and <i>C. elegans</i>. Protein kinases and associated domains, 169 proteins containing a disease resistance protein signature, and the Toll/IL-1R (TIR) domain, a component of pathogen recognition molecules<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Bent, A. F.  et al. RPS2 of Arabidopsis thaliana a leucine-rich repeat class of plant disease resistance genes. Science  265, 1856–1860 (1994)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2046">26</a></sup>, are also relatively abundant. This suggests that pathways transducing signals in response to pathogens and diverse environmental cues are more abundant in plants than in other organisms.</p><p>The RING zinc finger domain is relatively over-represented in <i>Arabidopsis </i> compared with yeast, <i>Drosophila</i> and <i>C. elegans</i>, whereas the F-box domain is over-represented as compared with yeast and <i>Drosophila </i> only. These domains are involved in targeting proteins to the proteasome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Skowyra, D.  et al. F box proteins are receptors that recruit phsphorylated substrates to the SCF ubiquitin-ligase complex. Cell 91, 209–219 (1997)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2065">27</a></sup> and ubiquitinylation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Joazeiro, C. A. P. &amp; Weissman, A. M. RING finger proteins: mediators of ubiquitin ligase activity. Cell  102, 549–552 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2070">28</a></sup> pathways of protein degradation, respectively. In plants many processes such as hormone and defence responses, light signalling, and circadian rhythms and pattern formation use F-box function to direct negative regulators to the ubiquitin degradation pathway. This mode of regulation appears to be more prevalent in plants and may account for a higher representation of the F box than in <i>Drosophila</i> and for the over-representation of the ubiquitin domain in the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome. RING finger domain proteins in general have a role in ubiquitin protein ligases, indicating that proteasome-mediated degradation is a more widespread mode of regulation in plants than in other kingdoms.</p><p>Most functions identified by protein domains are conserved in similar proportions in the <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>S. cerevisiae</i>, <i>Drosophila</i> and <i> C. elegans</i> genomes, pointing to many ubiquitous eukaryotic pathways. These are illustrated by comparing the list of human disease genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Rubin, G. M.  et al. Comparative genomics of the eukaryotes. Science  287, 2204–2215 ( 2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2095">29</a></sup> to the complete <i>Arabidopsis</i> gene set using BLASTP. Out of&nbsp;289 human disease genes, 139 (48%) had hits in <i>Arabidopsis</i> using&nbsp;a&nbsp;BLASTP threshold <i>E</i> &lt; 10<sup>-10</sup>. Sixty-nine (24%) exceeded&nbsp;an <i>E </i>&lt; 10<sup>-40</sup> threshold, and 26 (9.3%) had scores better than <i>E</i> &lt; 10<sup>-100</sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Tab3">Table 3</a>). There are at least 17 human disease genes more similar to <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes than yeast, <i>Drosophila </i> or <i>C. elegans</i> genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Tab3">Table&nbsp;3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes with similarities to human disease genes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/35048692/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>This analysis shows that, although numerous families of proteins are shared between all eukaryotes, plants contain roughly 150 unique protein families. These include transcription factors, structural proteins, enzymes and proteins of unknown function. Members of the families of genes common to all eukaryotes have undergone substantial increases or decreases in their size in <i>Arabidopsis </i>. Finally, the transfer of a relatively small number of cyanobacteria-related genes from a putative endosymbiotic ancestor of the plastid has added to the diversity of protein structures found in plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Genome organization and duplication "><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Genome organization and duplication </h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>The <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome sequence provides a complete view of chromosomal organization and clues to its evolutionary history. Gene families organized in tandem arrays of two or more units have been described in <i>C. elegans </i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The C. elegans Sequencing Consortium. Sequence and analysis of the genome of C. elegans. Science  282, 2012–2018 ( 1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2167">1</a></sup> and <i>Drosophila</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Adams, M. D. The genome sequence of Drosophila melanogaster. Science  287, 2185–2195 ( 2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2173">2</a></sup>. Analysis of the <i> Arabidopsis</i> genome revealed 1,528 tandem arrays containing 4,140 individual genes, with arrays ranging up to 23 adjacent members (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig3">Fig.3 </a>). Thus 17% of all genes of <i>Arabidopsis</i> are arranged in tandem arrays.</p><p>Large segmental duplications were identified either by directly aligning chromosomal sequences or by aligning proteins and searching for tracts of conserved gene order. All five chromosomes were aligned to each other in both orientations using MUMmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Delcher, A. L.  et al. Alignment of whole genomes. Nucleic Acids Res.  27, 2369–2376 ( 1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2190">30</a></sup>, and the results were filtered to identify all segments at least 1,000 bp in length with at least 50% identity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> Fig. 1). These revealed 24 large duplicated segments of 100 kb or larger, comprising 65.6 Mb or 58% of the genome. The only duplicated segment in the centromeric regions was a 375-kb segment on chromosome 4. Many duplications appear to have undergone further shuffling, such as local inversions after the duplication event.</p><p>We used TBLASTX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mayer, K. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromsome 4 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 769– 777 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2200">5</a></sup> to identify collinear clusters of genes residing in large duplicated chromosomal segments. The duplicated regions encompass 67.9 Mb, 60% of the genome, slightly more than was found in the DNA-based alignment (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>), and these data extend earlier findings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lin, X. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 2 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 761– 768 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2207">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mayer, K. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromsome 4 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 769– 777 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2210">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Blanc, G. et al. Extensive duplication and reshuffling in the Arabidopsis genome. Plant Cell 12, 1093– 1102 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2213">31</a></sup>. The extent of sequence conservation of the duplicated genes varies greatly, with 6,303 (37%) of the 17,193 genes in the segments classified as highly conserved (<i>E</i> &lt; 10<sup>-30</sup>) and a further 1,705 (10%) showing less significant similarity up to <i>E</i> &lt; 10<sup>-5</sup>. The proportion of homologous genes in each duplicated segment also varies widely, between 20% and 47% for the highly conserved class of genes. In many cases, the number of copies of a gene and its counterpart differ (for example, one copy on one chromosome and multiple copies on the other; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information </a> Fig. 2); this could be due to either tandem duplication or gene loss after the segmental duplication.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Segmentally duplicated regions in the Arabidopsis genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Segmentally duplicated regions in the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="600" height="363"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Individual chromosomes are depicted as horizontal grey bars (with chromosome 1 at the top), centromeres are marked black. Coloured bands connect corresponding duplicated segments. Similarity between the rDNA repeats are excluded. Duplicated segments in reversed orientation are connected with twisted coloured bands. The scale is in megabases.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>What does the duplication in the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome tell us about the ancestry of the species? Polyploidy occurs widely in plants and is proposed to be a key factor in plant evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wendel, J. F. Genome evolution in polyploids. Plant Mol. Biol. 42 , 225–249 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2249">32</a></sup>. As the majority of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome is represented in duplicated (but not triplicated) segments, it appears most likely that <i>Arabidopsis</i>, like maize, had a tetraploid ancestor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Gaut, B. S. &amp; Doebley, J. F. DNA sequence evidence for the segmental allotetraploid origin of maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 94, 6809–6814 ( 1997)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2259">33</a></sup>. A comparative sequence analysis of <i> Arabidopsis</i> and tomato estimated that a duplication occurred <span class="stix">∼</span>112 Myr ago to form a tetraploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Ku, H. -M., Vision, T., Liu, J. &amp; Tanksley, S. D. Comparing sequenced segments of the tomato and Arabidopsis genomes: Large-scale duplication followed by selective gene loss creates a network of synteny. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 9121– 9126 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2267">34</a></sup>. The degrees of conservation of the duplicated segments might be due to divergence from an ancestral autotetraploid form, or might reflect differences present in an allotetraploid ancestor. It is also possible, however, that several independent segmental duplication events took place instead of tetraploid formation and stabilization.</p><p>The diploid genetics of <i>Arabidopsis</i> and the extensive divergence of the duplicated segments have masked its evolutionary history. The determination of <i>Arabidopsis</i> gene functions must therefore be pursued with the potential for functional redundancy taken into account. The long period of time over which genome stabilization has occurred has, however, provided ample opportunity for the divergence of the functions of genes that arose from duplications. </p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="Comparative analysis of Arabidopsis accessions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Comparative analysis of <i>Arabidopsis</i> accessions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>Comparing the multiple accessions of <i>Arabidopsis</i> allows us to identify commonly occurring changes in genome microstructure. It also enables the development of new molecular markers for genetic mapping. High rates of polymorphism between <i> Arabidopsis</i> accessions, including both DNA sequence and copy number of tandem arrays, are prevalent at loci involved in disease resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Noel, L. et al. Pronounced intraspecific haplotype divergence at the RPP5 complex disease resistance locus of Arabidopsis. Plant Cell  11, 2099–2111 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2299">35</a></sup>. This has been observed for other plant species, and such loci are thought to serve as templates for illegitimate recombination to create new pathogen response specificities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Ellis, J., Dodds, P. &amp; Pryor, T. Structure, function, and evolution of plant disease resistance genes. Trends Plant Sci. 3, 278– 284 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2303">36</a></sup>. We carried out a comparative analysis between 82 Mb of the genome sequence of <i>Arabidopsis</i> accession Columbia (Col-0) and 92.1 Mb of non-redundant low-pass (twofold redundant) sequence data of the genomic DNA of accession Landsberg <i>erecta</i> (L<i> er</i>). We identified two classes of differences between the sequences: single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), and insertion–deletions (InDels). As we used high stringency criteria, our results represent a minimum estimate of numbers of polymorphisms between the two genomes.</p><p>In total, we detected 25,274 SNPs, representing an average density of 1 SNP per 3.3 kb. Transitions (A/T–G/C) represented 52.1% of the SNPs, and transversions accounted for the remainder: 17.3% for A/T–T/A, 22.7% for A/T–C/G and 7.9% for C/G–G/C. In total, we detected 14,570 InDels at an average spacing of 6.1 kb. They ranged from 2 bp to over 38 kilobase-pairs, although 95% were smaller than 50 bp. Only 10% of the InDels were co-located with simple sequence repeats identified with the program Sputnik. An analysis of 416 relative insertions greater than 250 bp in Col-0 showed that 30% matched transposon-related proteins, indicating that a substantial proportion of the large InDels are the result of transposon insertion or excision. Many InDels contained entire active genes not related to transposons. Half of such genes absent from corresponding positions in the Col-0 sequence were found elsewhere on the genome of L<i>er</i>. This indicates that genes have been transferred to new genomic locations.</p><p>Gene structures are often affected by small InDels and SNPs. The positions of SNPs and InDels were mapped relative to 87,427 exons and 70,379 introns annotated in the Col-0 sequence. SNPs were found in exons, introns and intergenic regions at frequencies of 1 SNP per 3.1, 2.2 and 3.5 kb, respectively. The frequencies for InDels were 1 per 9.3, 3.1 and 4.3 kb, respectively. Polymorphisms were detected in 7% of exons, and alter the spliced sequences of 25% of the predicted genes. For InDels in exons, insertion lengths divisible by three are prevalent for small insertions (&lt; 50 bp), indicating that many proteins can withstand small insertions or deletions of amino acids without loss of function.</p><p>Our analyses show that sequence polymorphisms between accessions of <i> Arabidopsis</i> are common, and that they occur in both coding and non-coding regions. We found evidence for the relocation of genes in the genome, and for changes in the complement of transposable elements. The data presented here are available at http://www.arabidopsis.org/cereon/.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Comparison of Arabidopsis and other plant genera"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Comparison of <i>Arabidopsis</i> and other plant genera</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>Comparative genetic mapping can reveal extensive conservation of genome organization between closely related species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Tanksley, S. D.  et al. High density molecular linkage maps of the tomato and potato genomes. Genetics 132, 1141– 1160 (1992)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2345">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Moore, G., Devos, K. M., Wang, Z. &amp; Gale, M. D. Grasses, line up and form a circle. Curr. Biol. 5, 737 –739 (1995)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2348">38</a></sup>. The comparative analysis of plant genome microstructure reveals much about the evolution of plant genomes and provides unprecedented opportunities for crop improvement by establishing the detailed structures of, and relationships between, the genomes of crops and <i>Arabidopsis</i>.</p><p>The lineages leading to <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>Capsella rubella</i> (shepherd' s purse) diverged between 6.2 and 9.8 Myr ago, and the gene content and genome organization of <i>C. rubella</i> is very similar to that of <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Acarkan, A., Rossberg, M., Koch, M. &amp; Schmidt, R. Comparative genome analysis reveals extensive conservation of genome organisation for Arabidopsis thaliana and Capsella rubella. Plant J.  23, 55–62 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2369">39</a></sup>, including the large-scale duplications. Alignment of <i>Arabidopsis</i> complementary DNA and EST sequences with genomic DNA sequences of <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>C. rubella</i> showed conservation of exon length and intron positions. Coding sequences predicted from these alignments differed from the annotated <i>Arabidopsis</i> gene sequences in two out of five cases.</p><p>The ancestral lineages of <i>Arabidopsis</i> and the <i>Brassica</i> (cabbage and mustard) genera diverged 12.2–19.2 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Cavell, A., Lydiate, D., Parkin, I., Dean, C. &amp; Trick, M. A 30 centimorgan segment of Arabidopsis thaliana chromosome 4 has six collinear homologues within the Brassica napus genome.  Genome 41, 62–69 ( 1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2395">40</a></sup>. <i> Brassica</i> genes show a high level of nucleotide conservation with their <i> Arabidopsis</i> orthologues, typically more than 85% in coding regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Cavell, A., Lydiate, D., Parkin, I., Dean, C. &amp; Trick, M. A 30 centimorgan segment of Arabidopsis thaliana chromosome 4 has six collinear homologues within the Brassica napus genome.  Genome 41, 62–69 ( 1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2406">40</a></sup>. The structure of <i>Brassica</i> genomes resembles that of <i> Arabidopsis</i>, but with extensive triplication and rearrangement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="O'Neill, C. &amp; Bancroft, I. Comparative physical mapping of segments of the genome of Brassica oleracea var alboglabra that are homologous to sequenced regions of the chromosomes 4 and 5 of Arabidopsis thaliana. Plant J. 23, 233– 243 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2416">41</a></sup>, and extensive divergence of microstructure (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information </a> Fig. 3). The divergence between the genomes of <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i> Brassica oleracea</i> is in striking contrast to that observed between <i> Arabidopsis</i> and <i>C. rubella</i>, although the time since divergence is only twofold greater. This accelerated rate of change in triplicated segments of the genome of <i>B. oleracea</i> indicates that polyploidy fosters rapid chromosomal evolution.</p><p>The <i>Arabidopsis</i> and tomato lineages diverged roughly 150 Myr ago, and comparative sequence analysis of segments of their genomes has revealed complex relationships<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Ku, H. -M., Vision, T., Liu, J. &amp; Tanksley, S. D. Comparing sequenced segments of the tomato and Arabidopsis genomes: Large-scale duplication followed by selective gene loss creates a network of synteny. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 9121– 9126 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2445">34</a></sup>. Four regions of the <i>Arabidopsis </i> genome are related to each other and to one region in the tomato genome, suggesting that two rounds of duplication may have occurred in the <i>Arabidopsis </i> lineage. The extensive duplication described here supports the proposal that the more recent of these duplications, estimated to have occurred <span class="stix">∼</span>112 Myr ago, was the result of a polyploidization event. The lineages of <i>Arabidopsis </i> and rice diverged <span class="stix">∼</span>200 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wolfe, K. H., Gouy, M., Yang, Y. -W., Sharp, P. M. &amp; Li, W. -H. Date of the monocot-dicot divergence estimated from the chloroplast DNA sequence data. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA  86, 6201–6205 (1989)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2459">42</a></sup>. Three regions of the genome of <i>Arabidopsis</i> were related to each other and to one region in the rice genome, providing further evidence for multiple duplication events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="van Dodeweerd, A. -M.  et al. Identification and analysis of homologous segments of the genomes of rice and Arabidopsis thaliana. Genome  42, 887–892 (1999)" href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2466">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Mayer, K. Sequence level analysis of homologous segments of the genomes of rice and  Arabidopsis thaliana. Genome Res. (submitted)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2469">44</a></sup>.</p><p>The frequent occurrence of tandem gene duplications and the apparent deletion of single genes, or small groups of adjacent genes, from duplicated regions suggests that unequal crossing over may be a key mechanism affecting the evolution of plant genome microstructure. However, the segmental inversions and gene translocations in the genomes of both rice and <i>B. oleracea</i> that are not found in <i>Arabidopsis</i> indicate that additional mechanisms may be involved<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Cavell, A., Lydiate, D., Parkin, I., Dean, C. &amp; Trick, M. A 30 centimorgan segment of Arabidopsis thaliana chromosome 4 has six collinear homologues within the Brassica napus genome.  Genome 41, 62–69 ( 1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2483">40</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Integration of the three genomes in the plant cell"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Integration of the three genomes in the plant cell</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>The three genomes in the plant cell—those of the nucleus, the plastids (chloroplasts) and the mitochondria—differ markedly in gene number, organization and stability. Plastid genes are densely packed in an order highly conserved in all plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Sato, S. Complete structure of the chloroplast genome of Arabidopsis thaliana. DNA Research 6, 283–290 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2495">45</a></sup>, whereas mitochondrial genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Unseld, M., Marienfeld, J., Brandt, P. &amp; Brennicke, A. The mitochondrial genome in Arabidopsis thaliana contains 57 genes in 366,924 nucleotides. Nature Genet. 15, 57–61 (1997)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2499">46</a></sup> are widely dispersed and subjected to extensive recombination. </p><p>Organellar genomes are remnants of independent organisms—plastids are derived from the cyanobacterial lineage and mitochondria from the α-Proteobacteria. The remaining genes in plastids include those that encode subunits of the photosystem and the electron transport chain, whereas the genes in mitochondria encode essential subunits of the respiratory chain. Both organelles contain sets of specific membrane proteins that, together with housekeeping proteins, account for 61% of the genes in the chloroplast and 88 % in the mitochondrion (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Tab4">Table 4</a>). The balances are involved in transcription and translation.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 General features of genes encoded by the three genomes in <i>Arabidopsis </i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/35048692/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The number of proteins encoded in the nucleus likely to be found in organelles was predicted using default settings on TargetP (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Many nuclear gene products that are targeted to either (or both) organelles were originally encoded in the organelle genomes and were transferred to the nuclear genome during evolutionary history. A large number also appear to be of eukaryotic origin, with functions such as protein import components, which were probably not required by the free-living ancestors of the endosymbionts. </p><p>To identify nuclear genes of possible organellar ancestry, we compared all predicted <i>Arabidopsis</i> proteins to all proteins from completed genomes including those from plastids and mitochondria (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> Table 2). This search identified proteins encoded by the <i> Arabidopsis</i> nuclear genome that are most similar to proteins encoded by other species’ organelle genomes (14 mitochondrial and 44 plastid). These represent organelle-to-nuclear gene transfers that have occurred sometime after the divergence of the organelle-containing lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Palmer, J. D.  et al. Dynamic evolution of plant mitochondrial genomes: mobile genes and introns and highly variable mutation rates. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 6960–6966 ( 2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2537">47</a></sup>. There is a great excess of nuclear encoded proteins most similar to proteins from the cyanobacteria <i>Synechocystis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information </a> Fig. 4; 806 <i>Arabidopsis</i> predicted proteins matching 404 different <i> Synechocystis</i> proteins, providing further evidence of a genome duplication). These 806 <i>Arabidopsis</i> predicted proteins, and many others of greatly diverse function, are possibly of plastid descent. Through searches against proteins from other cyanobacteria (with incompletely sequenced genomes), we identified 69 additional genes of possibly plastid descent. Only 25% of these putatively plastid-derived proteins displayed a target peptide predicted by TargetP, indicating potential cytoplasmic functions for most of these genes. </p><p>The difference between predicted plastid-targeted and predicted plastid-derived genes indicates that there is a probable overestimation by <i>ab initio</i> targeting prediction methods and a lack of resolution with respect to destination organelles, the possible extensive divergence of some endosymbiont-derived genes in the nuclear genome, the co-opting of nuclear genes for targeting to organelles, and cytoplasmic functions for cyanobacteria-derived proteins.
