Clearly more refined tools and extensive experimentation is required to catalogue plastid proteins.</p><p>The transfer of genes between genomes still continues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> Table 3). Plastid DNA insertions in the nucleus (17 insertions totalling 11 kb) contain full-length genes encoding proteins or tRNAs, fragments of genes and an intron as well as intergenic regions. Subsequent reshuffling in the nucleus is illustrated by the <i>atpH</i> gene, which was originally transferred completely, but is now in two pieces separated by 2 kb. The 13 small mitochondrial DNA insertions total 7 kb in addition to the large insertion close to the centromere of chromosome 2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Meinke, D. W., Cherry, J. M., Dean, C., Rounsley, S. D. &amp; Koornneef, M. Arabidopsis thaliana: a model plant for genome analysis. Science 282, 662– 665 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2572">3</a>). The high level of recombination in the mitochondrial genome may account for these events.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Transposable elements"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Transposable elements</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Transposons, which were originally identified in maize by Barbara McClintock, have been found in all eukaryotes and prokaryotes. A subset of transposons replicate through an RNA intermediate (class I), whereas others move directly through a DNA form (class II). Transposons are further classified by similarity either between their mobility genes or between their terminal and/or internal motifs, as well as by the size and sequence of their target site. Internally deleted elements can often be mobilized in <i>trans</i> by fully functional elements.</p><p>Transposons in <i>Arabidopsis</i> account for at least 10% of the genome, or about one-fifth of the intergenic DNA. The <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome has a wealth of class I (2,109) and II (2,203) elements, including several new groups (1,209 elements; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> Table 4). Mobile histories for many elements were obtained by identifying regions of the genome with significant similarity to ‘empty’ target sites (RESites) thus providing high-resolution information concerning the termini and target site duplications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Le, Q. -H.  et al. Transposon diversity in Arabidopsis thaliana.  Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 7376– 7381 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2599">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Yu, Z., Wright, S. &amp; Bureau, T. Mutator-like elements (MULEs) in Arabidopsis thaliana: Structure, diversity and evolution. Genetics (in the press)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2602">49</a></sup>. These regions were readily detected because of the propensity of transposons to integrate into repeats and because of duplications in the genome sequence. In several cases, genes appear to have been included as ‘passengers’ in transposable units<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Le, Q. -H.  et al. Transposon diversity in Arabidopsis thaliana.  Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 7376– 7381 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2606">48</a></sup>. In some cases, shared sequence similarity, coding capacity and RESites attest to recent activity of transposable elements in the <i>Arabidopsis </i> genome. Only about 4% of the complete elements identified correspond to an EST, however, suggesting that most are not transcribed.</p><p>Transposable elements found in many other plant genomes are well represented in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, including <i>copia</i>- and <i>gypsy</i>-like long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons, long interspersal nuclear elements (LINEs); short interspersed nuclear elements (SINEs), <i>hobo</i>/<i>Activator </i>/Tam3 (<i>hA</i>T)-like elements, CACTA-like elements and miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements (MITES). Although usually small in size, some larger <i> Tourist</i>-like MITEs contain open reading frames (ORFs) with similarity to the transposases of bacterial insertion sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Le, Q. -H.  et al. Transposon diversity in Arabidopsis thaliana.  Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 7376– 7381 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2639">48</a></sup>. <i> Basho</i> and many <i>Mutator</i>-like elements (MULEs), first discovered in the <i>Arabidopsis</i> sequence, represent structurally unique transposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Le, Q. -H.  et al. Transposon diversity in Arabidopsis thaliana.  Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 7376– 7381 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2653">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Yu, Z., Wright, S. &amp; Bureau, T. Mutator-like elements (MULEs) in Arabidopsis thaliana: Structure, diversity and evolution. Genetics (in the press)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2656">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Feschotte, C. &amp; Mouches, C. Evidence that a family of miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements (MITEs) from the Arabidopsis thaliana  genome has arisen from a pogo-like DNA transposon. Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 730–737 ( 2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2659">50</a></sup>. <i>Basho</i> elements have a target site preference for mononucleotide ‘A’ and wide distribution among plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Le, Q. -H.  et al. Transposon diversity in Arabidopsis thaliana.  Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 7376– 7381 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2666">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Martienssen, R. Transposons, DNA methylation and gene control. Trends Genet.  14, 263–264 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2669">51</a></sup>. MULEs exhibit a high level of sequence diversity and members of most groups lack long terminal inverted repeats (TIRs). Phylogenetic analysis of the <i> Arabidopsis</i> MURA-like transposases suggests that TIR-containing MULEs are more closely related to one another than to MULEs lacking TIRs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Yu, Z., Wright, S. &amp; Bureau, T. Mutator-like elements (MULEs) in Arabidopsis thaliana: Structure, diversity and evolution. Genetics (in the press)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2676">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Singer, T., Yordan, C. &amp; Martienssen, R. Robertson's Mutator transposons in Arabidopsis  are regulated by the chromatin-remodeling gene Decrease in DNA Methylation (DDM1). Genes Dev. (in the press)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2679">52</a></sup>. </p><p>For many plants with large genomes, class I retrotransposons contribute most of the nucleotide content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="SanMiguel, P.  et al. Nested retrotransposons in the intergenic regions of the maize genome. Science 274, 765– 768 (1996)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2686">53</a></sup>. In the small <i>Arabidopsis </i> genome, class I elements are less abundant and primarily occupy the centromere. In contrast, <i>Basho</i> elements and class II transposons such as MITEs and MULEs predominate on the periphery of pericentromeric domains (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig5"> Fig. 5</a>). In class II transposons, MULEs and CACTA elements are clustered near centromeres and heterochromatic knobs, whereas MITEs and <i>hA</i>T elements have a less pronounced bias. The distribution pattern of transposable elements observed in <i>Arabidopsis</i> may reflect different types of pericentromeric heterochromatin regions and may be similar to those found in animals.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Distribution of class I, II and Basho transposons in Arabidopsis  chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Distribution of class I, II and <i>Basho</i> transposons in <i>Arabidopsis </i> chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="600" height="831"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The frequency of class I retroelements (green), class II DNA transposons (blue) and <i>Basho</i> elements (purple) are shown at 100-kb intervals along the five chromosomes of <i>Arabidopsis</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Numerous centromeric satellite repeats are located between each&nbsp;chromosome arm and have not yet been sequenced, but are&nbsp;represented in part by unanchored BAC contigs (R. Martienssen and M.&nbsp;Marra, unpublished data). End sequence suggests that these domains contain many more class I than class II elements, consistent with the distribution reported here (K. Lemcke and R. Martienssen, unpublished data). We do not know the significance of the apparent paucity of elements in telomeric regions and in the region flanking the rDNA repeats on chromosome 4 (but not on chromosome 2).</p><p>Overall, transposon-rich regions are relatively gene-poor and have lower rates of recombination and EST matches, indicating a correlation between low gene expression, high transposon density and low recombination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Martienssen, R. Transposons, DNA methylation and gene control. Trends Genet.  14, 263–264 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2735">51</a></sup>. The role of transposons in genome organization and chromosome structure can now be addressed in&nbsp;a model organism known to undergo DNA methylation and other forms of chromatin modification thought to regulate transposition<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Singer, T., Yordan, C. &amp; Martienssen, R. Robertson's Mutator transposons in Arabidopsis  are regulated by the chromatin-remodeling gene Decrease in DNA Methylation (DDM1). Genes Dev. (in the press)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2739">52</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="rDNA, telomeres and centromeres "><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">rDNA, telomeres and centromeres </h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Nucleolar organizers (NORs) contain arrays of unit repeats encoding the 18S, 5.8S and 25S ribosomal RNA genes and are transcribed by RNA polymerase I. Together with 5S RNA, which is transcribed by RNA polymerase III, these rRNAs form the structural and catalytic cores of cytoplasmic ribosomes. In <i> Arabidopsis</i>, the NORs juxtapose the telomeres of chromosomes 2 and 4, and comprise uninterrupted 18S, 5.8S and 25S units all orientated on the chromosomes in the same direction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Copenhaver, G. P. &amp; Pikaard, C. S. Two-dimensional RFLP analyses reveal megabase-sized clusters of rRNA gene variants in  Arabidopsis thaliana, suggesting local spreading of variants as the mode for gene homogenization during concerted evolution. Plant J.  9, 273–282 (1996)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2754">54</a></sup>. In contrast, the 5S rRNA genes are localized to heterogeneous arrays in the centromeric regions of chromosomes 3, 4 and 5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Fransz, P.  et al. Cytogenetics for the model system Arabidopsis thaliana . Plant J. 13, 867– 876 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2757">55</a>; and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig6">Fig.6 </a>). Both NORs are roughly 3.5–4.0 megabase-pairs and comprise <span class="stix">∼</span>350–400 highly methylated rRNA gene units, each <span class="stix">∼</span>10 kb (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference  54" title="Copenhaver, G. P. &amp; Pikaard, C. S. Two-dimensional RFLP analyses reveal megabase-sized clusters of rRNA gene variants in  Arabidopsis thaliana, suggesting local spreading of variants as the mode for gene homogenization during concerted evolution. Plant J.  9, 273–282 (1996)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2763"> 54</a>). The sequence between the euchromatic arms and NORs has been determined. Elsewhere in the genome, only one other 18S, 5.8S, 25S rRNA gene unit was identified in centromere 3. Although minor variations in sequence length and composition occur in the NOR repeats, these variants are highly clustered, supporting a model of sequence maintenance through concerted evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Fransz, P.  et al. Cytogenetics for the model system Arabidopsis thaliana . Plant J. 13, 867– 876 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2768">55</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Predicted centromere composition."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Predicted centromere composition.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="600" height="267"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Genetically defined centromere boundaries are indicated by filled circles; fully and partially assembled BAC sequences are represented by solid and dashed black lines, respectively. Estimates of repeat sizes within the centromeres were derived from consideration of repeat copy number, physical mapping and cytogenetic assays.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>Arabidopsis</i> telomeres are composed of CCCTAAA repeats and average <span class="stix">∼</span>2–3 kb (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Richards, E. J. &amp; Ausubel, F. M. Isolation of a higher eukarotic telomere from Arabidopsis thaliana.  Cell 53, 127–136 ( 1988)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2784">56</a>). For TEL4N (telomere 4 North), consensus repeats are adjacent to the NOR; the remaining telomeres are typically separated from coding sequences by repetitive subtelomeric regions measuring less than 4 kb. Imperfect telomere-like arrays of up to 24 kb are found elsewhere in the genome, particularly near centromeres. These arrays might affect the expression of nearby genes and may have resulted from ancient rearrangements, such as inversions of the chromosome arms.</p><p>Centromere DNA mediates chromosome attachment to the meiotic and mitotic spindles and often forms dense heterochromatin. Genetic mapping of the regions that confer centromere function provided the markers necessary to precisely place BAC clones at individual centromeres<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Copenhaver, G. P.  et al. Genetic definition and sequence analysis of Arabidopsis  centromeres. Science 286, 2468– 2474 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2791">17</a></sup>; 69 clones were targeted for sequencing, resulting in over 5 Mb of DNA sequence from the centromeric regions. The unsequenced regions of centromeres are composed primarily of long, homogeneous arrays that were characterized previously with physical<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Round, E. K., Flowers, S. K. &amp; Richards, E. J. Arabidopsis thaliana centromere regions: genetic map positions and repetitive DNA structure. Genome Res.  7, 1045–1053 (1997)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2795">57</a></sup> and genetic mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Copenhaver, G. P.  et al. Genetic definition and sequence analysis of Arabidopsis  centromeres. Science 286, 2468– 2474 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2799">17</a></sup> and contain over 3 Mb of repetitive arrays, including the 180-bp repeats and 5S rDNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Martienssen, R. Transposons, DNA methylation and gene control. Trends Genet.  14, 263–264 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2803">51</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>).</p><p><i>Arabidopsis</i> centromeres, like those of many higher eukaryotes, contain numerous repetitive elements including retroelements, transposons, microsatellites and middle repetitive DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Copenhaver, G. P.  et al. Genetic definition and sequence analysis of Arabidopsis  centromeres. Science 286, 2468– 2474 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2815">17</a></sup>. These repeats are rare in the euchromatic arms and often most abundant in pericentromeric DNA. The repeats, affinity for DNA-binding dyes, dense methylation patterns and inhibition of homologous recombination indicate that the centromeric regions are highly heterochromatic, and such regions are generally viewed as very poor environments for gene expression. Unexpectedly, we found at least 47 expressed genes encoded in the genetically defined centromeres of <i>Arabidopsis</i> (http://preuss.bsd.uchicago.edu/arabidopsis.genome.html). In several cases, these genes reside on islands of unique sequence flanked by repetitive arrays, such as 180-bp or 5S rDNA repeats. Among the genes encoded in the centromeres are members of 11 of the 16 functional categories that comprise the proteome. The centromeres are not subject to recombination; consequently, genes residing in these regions probably exhibit unique patterns of molecular evolution.</p><p>The function of higher eukaryotic centromeres may be specified by proteins that bind to centromere DNA, by epigenetic modifications, or by secondary or higher order structures. A pairwise comparison of the non-repetitive portions of all five centromeres showed they share limited (1–7%) sequence similarity. Forty-one families of small, conserved centromere sequences (AtCCS, see http://preuss.bsd.uchicago.edu/arabidopsis.genome.html) are enriched in the centromeric and pericentromeric regions and differ from sequences found in the centromeres of other eukaryotes. Molecular and genetic assays will be required to determine whether these conserved motifs nucleate <i> Arabidopsis</i> centromere activity. Apart from the AtCCS sequences, most centromere DNA is not shared between chromosomes, complicating efforts to derive clear evolutionary relationships. In contrast, genetic and cytological assays indicate that homologous centromeres are highly conserved among <i> Arabidopsis</i> accessions, albeit subject to rearrangements such as inversions to form knobs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mayer, K. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromsome 4 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 769– 777 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2832">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="The CSHL/WUGSC/PEB Arabidopsis Sequencing Consortium. The complete sequence of a heterochromatic island from a higher eukaryote. Cell 100, 377– 386 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2835">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Fransz, P. F.  et al. Integrated cytogenetic map of chromosome arm 4S of A. thaliana : Structural organization of heterochromatic knob and centromere region. Cell 100, 367–376 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2838">59</a></sup> and insertions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lin, X. et al. Sequence and analysis of chromosome 2 of the plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 402, 761– 768 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2842">4</a></sup>. Further investigation of centromere DNA promises to yield information on the evolutionary forces that act in regions of limited recombination, as well as an improved understanding of the role of DNA sequence patterns in chromosome segregation. </p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Membrane transport"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Membrane transport</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Transporters in the plasma and intracellular membranes of <i>Arabidopsis </i> are responsible for the acquisition, redistribution and compartmentalization of organic nutrients and inorganic ions, as well as for the efflux of toxic compounds and metabolic end products, energy and signal transduction, and turgor generation. Previous genomic analyses of membrane transport systems in <i>S.&nbsp;cerevisiae</i> and <i>C. elegans</i> led to the identification of over 100 distinct families of membrane transporters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Paulsen, I. T., Nguyen, L., Sliwinski, M. K., Rabus, R. &amp; Saier, M. H. Jr Microbial genome analyses: comparative transport capabilities in eighteen prokaryotes.  J. Mol. Biol. 301, 75–101 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2863">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Paulsen, I. T., Sliwinski, M. K., Nelissen, B., Goffeau, A. &amp; Saier, M. H. Jr Unified inventory of established and putative transporters encoded within the complete genome of Saccharomyces cerevisiae. FEBS Lett. 430, 116–125 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2866">61</a></sup>. We compared membrane transport processes between <i>Arabidopsis</i>, animals, fungi and prokaryotes, and identified over 600 predicted membrane transport systems in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (http://www-biology.ucsd.edu/<span class="stix">∼</span>ipaulsen/transport/), a similar number to that of <i>C. elegans</i> (<span class="stix">∼</span>700 transporters) and over twofold greater than either <i>S.&nbsp;cerevisiae</i> or <i>E. coli</i> (<span class="stix">∼</span>300 transporters).</p><p>We compared the transporter complement of <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>C.&nbsp;elegans </i> and <i>S. cerevisiae</i> in terms of energy coupling mechanisms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig7">Fig. 7a</a>). Unlike animals, which use a sodium ion P-type ATPase pump to generate an electrochemical gradient across the plasma membrane, plants and fungi use a proton P-type ATPase pump to form a large membrane potential (-250 mV)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Hirsch, R. E., Lewis, B. D, Spalding, E. P. &amp; Sussman, M. R. A role for the AKT1 potassium channel in plant nutrition. Science  280, 918–921 ( 1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2901">62</a></sup>. Consequently, plant secondary transporters are typically coupled to protons rather than to sodium<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Slayman, C. L. &amp; Slayman, C. W. Depolarization of the plasma membrane of Neurospora during active transport of glucose: evidence for a proton-dependent cotransport system. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 71, 1035–1939 ( 1974)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2906">63</a></sup>. Compared with <i>C. elegans, Arabidopsis</i> has a surprisingly high percentage of primary ATP-dependent transporters (12% and 21% of transporters, respectively), reflecting increased numbers of P-type ATPases involved in metal ion transport and ABC ATPases proposed to be involved in sequestering unusual metabolites and drugs in the vacuole or in other intracellular compartments. These processes may be necessary for pathogen defence and nutrient storage.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Comparison of the transport capabilities of Arabidopsis,  C. elegans and S.&nbsp;cerevisiae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 7: Comparison of the transport capabilities of <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i> C. elegans</i> and <i>S.&nbsp;cerevisiae</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig7_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2F35048692/MediaObjects/41586_2000_Article_BF35048692_Fig7_HTML.jpg" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="600" height="753"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Pie charts show the percentage of transporters in each organism according to bioenergetics (<b>a</b>) and substrate specificity (<b>b</b>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/35048692/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>About 15% of the transporters in <i>Arabidopsis</i> are channel proteins, five times more than in any single-celled organism but half the number in <i> C. elegans</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig7">Fig. 7b</a>). Almost half of the <i>Arabidopsis </i> channel proteins are aquaporins, and <i>Arabidopsis</i> has 10-fold more Mfamily major intrinsic protein (MIP) family water channels than any other sequenced organism. This abundance emphasizes the importance of hydraulics in a wide range of plant processes, including sugar and nutrient transport into and out of the vasculature, opening of stomatal apertures, cell elongation and epinastic movements of leaves and stems. Although <i>Arabidopsis</i> has a diverse range of metal cation transporters, <i>C. elegans</i> has more, many of which function in cell–cell signalling and nerve signal transduction. <i> Arabidopsis</i> also possesses transporters for inorganic anions such as phosphate, sulphate, nitrate and chloride, as well as for metal cation channels that serve in signal transduction or cell homeostasis. Compared with other sequenced organisms, <i>Arabidopsis</i> has 10-fold more predicted peptide transporters, primarily of the proton-dependent oligopeptide transport (POT) family, emphasizing the importance of peptide transport or indicating that there is broader substrate specificity than previously realized. There are nearly 1,000 <i>Arabidopsis </i> genes encoding Ser/Thr protein kinases, suggesting that peptides may have an important role in plant signalling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Ryan, C. A. &amp; Pearce, G. Systemin: a polypeptide signal for plant defensive genes. Annu. Rev. Cell. Dev. Biol.  14, 1–17 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d33224e2972">64</a></sup>.</p><p>Virtually no transporters for carboxylates, such as lactate and pyruvate, were identified in the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome. About 12% of the transporters were predicted to be sugar transporters, mostly consisting of paralogues of the MFS family of hexose transporters. Notably, <i>S. cerevisiae</i>, <i> C. elegans</i> and most prokaryotes use APC&nbsp;family transporters as their principle means of amino-acid transport, but <i>Arabidopsis</i> appears to rely primarily on the AAAP family of amino-acid and auxin transporters. More than 10% of the transporters in <i>Arabidopsis</i> are homologous to drug efflux pumps; these probably represent transporters involved in the sequestration into vacuoles of xenobiotics, secondary metabolites, and breakdown products of chlorophyll.</p><p>Surprisingly, <i>Arabidopsis</i> has close homologues of the human ABC TAP transporters of antigenic peptides for presentation to the major histocompatability complex (MHC). In <i>Arabidopsis</i>, these transporters may be involved in peptide efflux, or more speculatively, in some form of cell-recognition response. <i> Arabidopsis</i> also has 10-fold more members of the multi-drug and toxin extrusion (MATE) family than any other sequenced organism; in bacteria, these transporters function as drug efflux pumps. Curiously, <i>Arabidopsis</i> has several homologues of the <i>Drosophila</i> RND transporter family Patched protein, which functions in segment polarity, and more than ten homologues of the <i>Drosophila</i> ABC family eye pigment transporters. In plants, these are presumably involved in intracellular sequestration of secondary metabolites. </p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="DNA repair and recombination"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">DNA repair and recombination</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>DNA repair and recombination pathways have many functions in different species such as maintaining genomic integrity, regulating mutation rates, chromosome segregation and recombination, genetic exchange within and between populations, and immune system development. Comparing the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome with other species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Eisen, J. A. &amp; Hanawalt, P. C. A phylogenomic study of DNA repair genes, proteins, and processes. Mutat. Res.  435, 171–213 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3028">65</a></sup> indicates that <i>Arabidopsis</i> has a similar set of DNA repair and recombination (RAR) genes to most other eukaryotes. The pathways represented include photoreactivation, DNA ligation, non-homologous end joining, base excision repair, mismatch excision repair, nucleotide excision repair and many aspects of DNA recombination (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1"> Supplementary Information</a> Table 5). The <i>Arabidopsis</i> RAR genes include homologues of many DNA repair genes that are defective in different human diseases (for example, hereditary breast cancer and non-polyposis colon cancer, xeroderma pigmentosum and Cockayne's syndrome).</p><p>One feature that sets <i>Arabidopsis</i> apart from other eukaryotes is the presence of additional homologues of many RAR genes. This is seen for almost every major class of DNA repair, including recombination (four RecA), DNA ligation (four DNA ligase I), photoreactivation (one class II photolyase and five class I photolyase homologues) and nucleotide excision repair (six RPA1, two RPA2, two Rad25, three TFB1 and four Rad23). This is most striking for genes with probable roles in base excision repair. <i>Arabidopsis</i> encodes 16 homologues of DNA base glycosylases (enzymes that recognize abnormal DNA bases and cleave them from the sugar-phosphate backbone)—more than any other species known. This includes several homologues of each of three families of alkylation damage base glycosylases: two of the <i>S. cerevisiae </i> MPG; six of the <i>E. coli</i> TagI; and two of the <i>E. coli</i> AlkA. <i> Arabidopsis</i> also encodes three homologues of the apurinic-apyrimidinic (AP) endonuclease Xth. AP endonucleases continue the base excision repair started by glycosylases by cleaving the DNA backbone at abasic sites.</p><p>Evolutionary analysis indicates that some of the extra copies of RAR genes in <i>Arabidopsis</i> originated through relatively recent gene duplications—because many of the sets of genes are more closely related to each other than to their homologues in any other species. As duplication is frequently accompanied by functional divergence, the duplicate (paralogous) genes may have different repair specificities or may have evolved functions that are outside RAR functions (as is the case for two of the five class I photolyase homologues, which function as blue-light receptors). In most cases, it is not known whether the paralogous gene copies have different functions. The presence of multiple paralogues might also allow functional redundancy or a greater repair or recombination capacity.</p><p>The multiplicity of RAR genes in <i>Arabidopsis</i> is also partly due to the transfer of genes from the organellar genomes to the nucleus. Repair gene homologues that appear to be of chloroplast origin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> Tables 2 and 5) include the recombination proteins RecA, RecG and SMS, two class I photolyase homologues, Fpg, two MutS2 proteins, and the transcription-repair coupling factor Mfd. Two of these (RecA and Fpg) are involved in RAR functions in the plastid, suggesting that the others may be as well. The finding of an Mfd orthologue of cyanobacterial descent is surprising. In <i>E. coli</i>, Mfd couples nucleotide excision repair carried out by UvrABC to transcription, leading to the rapid repair of DNA damage on the transcribed strand of transcribed genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Selby, C. P. &amp; Sancar, A. Structure and function of transcription-repair coupling factor. Structural domains and binding properties. J. Biol. Chem. 270, 4882–4889 (1995)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3081">66</a></sup> The absence of orthologues of UvrABC in <i>Arabidopsis</i> renders the function of Mfd difficult to predict. The presence of Mfd but not UvrABC has been reported for only one other species, a bacterial endosymbiont of the pea aphid.</p><p>Other nuclear-encoded <i>Arabidopsis</i> DNA repair gene homologues are evolutionarily related to genes from α-Proteobacteria, and thus may be of mitochondrial descent. In particular, the six homologues of the alkyl-base glycosylase TagI appear to be the result of a large expansion in plants after transfer from the mitochondrial genome. Whether any of these TagI homologues function in the repair and maintenance of mitochondrial DNA has not been determined. More detailed phylogenetic analysis may reveal additional <i>Arabidopsis</i> RAR genes to be of organellar ancestry.</p><p>There are some notable absences of proteins important for RAR in other species, including alkyltransferases, MSH4, RPA3 and many components of TFIIH (TFB2, TFB3, TFB4, CCL1, Kin28). Nevertheless, <i>Arabidopsis</i> shows many similarities to the set of DNA repair genes found in other eukaryotes, and therefore offers an experimental system for determining the functions of many of these proteins, in part through characterization of mutants defective in DNA repair<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Britt, A. B. Molecular genetics of DNA repair in higher plants. Trends Plant Sci. 4, 20–25 ( 1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3104">67</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Gene regulation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Gene regulation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>Eukaryotic gene expression involves many nuclear proteins that modulate chromatin structure, contribute to the basal transcription machinery, or mediate gene regulation in response to developmental, environmental or metabolic cues. As predicted by sequence similarity, more than 3,000 such proteins may be encoded by the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome, suggesting that it has a comparable complexity of gene regulation to other eukaryotes. <i>Arabidopsis</i> has an additional level of gene regulation, however, with DNA methylation potentially mediating gene silencing and parental imprinting.</p><p>Plants have evolved several variations on chromatin remodelling proteins, such as the family of HD2 histone deacetylases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Dangl, M. Response to Aravind, L. &amp; Koonin, E. V. Second Family of Histone Deacetylases. Science 280, 1167 ( 1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3126">68</a></sup>. Although <i> Arabidopsis</i> possesses the usual number of SNF2-type chromatin remodelling ATPases, which regulate the expression of nearly all genes, there are significant structural differences between yeast and metazoan SNF2-type genes and their orthologues in <i>Arabidopsis</i>. DDM1, a member of the SNF2 superfamily, and MOM1, a gene with similarity to the SNF2 family, are involved in transcriptional gene silencing in <i>Arabidopsis</i>. MOM1 has no clear orthologue in fungal or metazoan genomes.</p><p>Consistent with its methylated DNA, <i>Arabidopsis</i> possesses eight&nbsp;DNA methyltransferases (DMTs). Two of the three types are&nbsp;orthologous to mammalian DMT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Cao, X. et al. Conserved plant genes with similarity to mammalian de novo DNA methyltransferases. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 4979–4984 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3145">69</a></sup> whereas one, chromomethyltransferase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Henikoff, S. &amp; Comai, L. A DNA methyltransferase homologue with a chromodomain exists in multiple polymorphic forms in Arabidopsis . Genetics 149, 307– 318 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3149">70</a></sup>, is unique to plants. No DMTs are found in yeast or <i>C. elegans</i>, although two DMT-like genes are found in <i>Drosophila</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Hung, M. -S.  et al. Drosophila proteins related to vertebrate DNA (5-cytosine) methyltransferases. Proc Natl Acad. Sci. USA 96, 11940–11945 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3158">71</a></sup>. <i>Arabidopsis </i> also encodes eight proteins with methyl-DNA-binding domains (MBDs). Despite lacking methylated DNA, <i>Drosophila</i> encodes four MBD proteins and <i> C. elegans</i> has two. These differences in chromatin components are likely to reflect&nbsp;important differences in chromatin-based regulatory control&nbsp;of gene expression in eukaryotes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> Table 6; http://Ag.Arizona.Edu/chromatin/chromatin.html).</p><p>The <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome encodes transcription machinery for the three nuclear DNA-dependent RNA polymerase systems typical of eukaryotes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1"> Supplementary Information</a> Table 6). Transcription by RNA polymerases II and III appears to involve the same machinery as is used in other eukarotes; however, most transcription factors for RNA polymerase I are not readily identified. Only two polymerase I regulators (other than polymerase subunits and TATA-binding protein) are apparent in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, namely homologues of yeast RRN3 and mouse TTF-1. All eukaryotes examined to date have distinct genes for the largest and second largest subunits of polymerase I, II and III. Unexpectedly, <i> Arabidopsis</i> has two genes encoding a fourth class of largest subunit and second-largest subunit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> Fig. 5). It will be interesting to determine whether the atypical subunits comprise a polymerase that has a plant-specific function. Four genes encoding single-subunit plastid or mitochondrial RNA polymerases have been identified in <i>Arabidopsis </i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> Table 6). Genes for the bacterial β-, β′- and α-subunits of RNA polymerase are also present, as are homologues of various σ-factors, and these proteins may regulate chloroplast gene expression. Mutations in the <i>Sde-1</i> gene, encoding RNA-dependent RNA polymerase (RdRp), lead to defective post-transcriptional gene silencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Dalmay, T., Hamilton, A. J., Rudd, S., Angell, S. &amp; Baulcombe, D. C. An RNA-dependent-RNA polymerase in Arabidopsis is required for post transcriptional gene silencing mediated by a transgene but not by a virus—the truth. Cell  101, 543–553 ( 2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3203">72</a></sup>. We also identified five more closely related RdRp genes.</p><p>Our analysis, using both similarity searches and domain matches, has identified 1,709 proteins with significant similarity to known classes of plant transcription factors classified by conserved DNA-binding domains. This analysis used a consistent conservative threshold that probably underestimates the size of families of diverse sequence. This class of protein is the least conserved among all classes of known proteins, showing only 8–23% similarity to transcription factors in other eukaryotes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). This reduced similarity is due to the absence of certain classes of transcription factors in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and large numbers of plant-specific transcription factors. We did not detect any members of several widespread families of transcription factors, such as the REL (Rel-like DNA-binding domain) homology region proteins, nuclear steroid receptors and forkhead-winged helix and POU (Pit-1, Oct- and Unc-8b) domain families of developmental regulators. Conversely, of 29 classes of <i>Arabidopsis</i> transcription factors, 16 appear to be unique to plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> Table 6). Several of these, such as the AP2/EREBP-RAV, NAC and ARF-AUX/IAA families, contain unique DNA-binding domains, whereas others contain plant-specific variants of more widespread domains, such as the DOF and WRKY zinc-finger families and the two-repeat MYB family.</p><p>Functional redundancy among members of large families of closely related transcription factors in <i>Arabidopsis</i> is a significant potential barrier to their characterization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Riechmann, J. L. &amp; Ratcliffe, O. J. A genomic perspective on plant transcription factors. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol.  3, 423–434 ( 2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3229">73</a></sup>. For example, in the SHATTERPROOF and SEPALLATA families of MADS box transcription factors, all genes must be defective to produce visible mutant phenotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Liljegren, S. J.  et al. SHATTERPROOF MADS-box genes control seed dispersal in  Arabidopsis. Nature 404, 766– 770 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3233">74</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Pelaz, S. et al. B and C floral organ identity functions require SEPALLATA MADS-box genes. Nature 405, 200– 203 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3236">75</a></sup>. These functionally redundant genes are found on the segmental duplications described above. Our analyses, together with the significant sequence similarity found in large families of transcription factors such as the R2R3-repeat MYB and WRKY families, suggest that strategies involving overexpression will be important in determining the functions of members of transcription factor families. </p><p><i>Arabidopsis</i> has two or over three times more transcription factors than identified in <i>Drosophila</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Rubin, G. M.  et al. Comparative genomics of the eukaryotes. Science  287, 2204–2215 ( 2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3247">29</a></sup> or <i>C.elegans</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The C. elegans Sequencing Consortium. Sequence and analysis of the genome of C. elegans. Science  282, 2012–2018 ( 1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3253">1</a></sup>, respectively. The significantly greater extent of segmental chromosomal and local tandem duplications in the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome generates larger gene families, including transcription factors. The partly overlapping functions defined for a few transcription factors are also likely to be much more widespread, implicating many sequence-related transcription factors in the same cellular processes. Finally, the expanded number of genes involved in metabolism, defence and environmental interaction in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/35048692#Fig2">Fig. 2a </a>), which have few counterparts in <i>Drosophila</i> and <i>C. elegans </i>, all require additional numbers and classes of transcription factors to integrate gene function in response to a vast range of developmental and environmental cues.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Cellular organization"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Cellular organization</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><p>Plant cells differ from animal cells in many features such as plastids, vacuoles, Golgi organization, cytoskeletal arrays, plasmodesmata linking cytoplasms of neighbouring cells, and a rigid polysaccharide-rich extracellular matrix—the cell wall. Because the cell wall maintains the position of a cell relative to its neighbours, both changes in cell shape and organized cell divisions, involving cytoskeleton reorganization and membrane vesicle targeting, have major roles in plant development. Plant cytokinesis is also unique in that&nbsp;the partitioning membrane is formed <i>de novo</i> by vesicle fusion. We compared the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome with those of <i>C. elegans, Drosophila</i> and yeast to glimpse the genetic basis of plant-cell-specific features.</p><p>The principal components of the plant cytoskeleton are microtubules (MTs) and actin filaments (AFs); intermediate filaments (IFs) have not been described in plants. <i>Arabidopsis</i> appears to lack genes for cytokeratin or vimentin, the main components of animal IFs, but has several variants of actin, α- and β-tubulin. The <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome also encodes homologues of chaperones that mediate the folding of tubulin and actin polypeptides in yeast and animal cells, such as the prefoldin and cytosolic chaperonin complexes and tubulin-folding cofactors. The dynamic stability of MTs and AFs is influenced by MT-associated proteins and actin-binding proteins, respectively, several of which are encoded by <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes. These include the MT-severing ATPase katanin, AF-crosslinking/bundling proteins, such as fimbrins and villins, and AF-disassembling proteins, such as profilin and actin-depolymerizing factor/cofilin. The <i>Arabidopsis</i> proteome appears to lack homologues of proteins that, in animal cells, link the actin cytoskeleton across the plasma membrane to the extracellular matrix, such as integrin, talin, spectrin, α-actinin, vitronectin or vinculin. This apparent lack of ‘anchorage’ proteins is consistent with the different composition of the cell wall and with a prominence of cortical MTs at the expense of cortical AFs in plant cells.</p><p>Plant-specific cytoskeletal arrays include interphase cortical MTs mediating cell shape, the preprophase band marking the cortical site of cell division, and the phragmoplast assisting in cytokinesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Canaday, J., Stoppin-Mellet, V., Mutterer, J., Lambert, A. M. &amp; Schmit, A. C. Higher plant cells: gamma-tubulin and microtubule nucleation in the absence of centrosomes. Microsc. Res. Technol. 49, 487–495 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3308">76</a></sup>. Although plant cells lack structural counterparts of the yeast spindle pole body and the animal centrosome, <i>Arabidopsis</i> has homologues of core components of the MT-nucleating γ-tubulin ring complex, such as γ-tubulin, Spc97/hGCP2 and Spc98/hGCP3. <i>Arabidopsis</i> has numerous motor molecules, both kinesins and dyneins with associated dynactin complex proteins, which are presumably involved in the dynamic organization of MTs and in transporting cargo along MT tracks. There are also myosin motors that may be involved in AF-supported organelle trafficking. Essential features of the eukaryotic cytoskeleton appear to be conserved in <i>Arabidopsis</i>.</p><p>The <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome encodes homologues of proteins involved in vesicle budding, including several ARFs and ARF-related small G-proteins, large but not small ARF GEFs (adenosine ribosylation factor on guanine nucleotide exchange factor), adapter proteins, and coat proteins of the COP and non-COP types. <i>Arabidopsis</i> also has homologues of proteins involved in vesicle docking and fusion, including SNAP receptors (SNAREs), <i>N</i>-ethylmaleimide-sensitive factor (NSF) and Cdc48-related ATPases, accessory proteins such as Sec1 and soluble NSF attachment protein (SNAP), and Rab-type GTPases. The large number of <i>Arabidopsis</i> SNAREs can be grouped by sequence similarity to yeast and animal counterparts involved in specific trafficking pathways, and some have been localized to the trans-Golgi and the pre-vacuolar pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Bassham, D. C. &amp; Raikhel, N. V. Unique features of the plant vacuolar sorting machinery. Curr. Opin. Cell Biol.  12, 491–495 ( 2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3336">77</a></sup>. <i> Arabidopsis</i> also has a receptor for retention of proteins in the endoplasmic reticulum, a cargo receptor for transport to the vacuole and several phragmoplastins related to animal dynamin GTPases. Thus, plant cells appear to use the same basic machinery for vesicle trafficking as yeast and animal cells.</p><p>Animal cells possess many functionally diverse small G-proteins of the Ras superfamily involved in signal transduction, AF reorganization, vesicle fusion and other processes. Surprisingly, <i>Arabidopsis</i> appears to lack genes for G-proteins of the Ras, Rho, Rac and Cdc42 subfamilies but has many Rab-type G-proteins involved in vesicle fusion and several Rop-type G-proteins, one of which has a role in actin organization of the tip-growing pollen tube<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Zheng, Z. L. &amp; Yang, Z. The Rrop GTPase switch turns on polar growth in pollen. Trends Plant Sci. 5, 298-303 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3351">78</a></sup>. The significance of this divergent amplification of different subfamilies of small G-proteins in plants and animals remains to be determined. </p><p><i>Arabidopsis</i> possesses cyclin-dependent kinases (CDKs), including a plant-specific Cdc2b kinase expressed in a cell-cycle-dependent manner, several cyclin subtypes, including a D-type cyclin that mediates cytokinin-stimulated cell-cycle progression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="den Boer, B. G. &amp; Murray, J. A. Triggering the cell cycle in plants. Trends Cell Biol. 10, 245–250 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3360">79</a></sup>, a retinoblastoma-related protein and components of the ubiquitin-dependent proteolytic pathway of cyclin degradation. In yeast and animal cells, chromosome condensation is mediated by condensins, sister chromatids are held together by cohesins such as Scc1, and metaphase–anaphase transition is triggered by separin/Esp1 endopeptidase proteolysis of Scc1 on APC-mediated degradation of its inhibitor, securin/Psd1. Related proteins are encoded by the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome. Thus, the basic machinery of cell-cycle progression, genome duplication and segregation appears to be conserved in plants. By contrast, entry into M phase, M-phase progression and cytokinesis seem to be modified in plant cells. <i>Arabidopsis</i> does not appear to have homologues of Cdc25 phosphatase, which activates Cdc2 kinase at the onset of mitosis, or of polo kinase, which regulates M-phase progression in yeast and animals. Conversely, plant-specific mitogen-actived protein (MAP) kinases appear to be involved in cytokinesis.</p><p>Cytokinesis partitions the cytoplasm of the dividing cell. Yeast and animal cells expand the membrane from the surface towards the centre in a cleavage process supported by septins and a contractile ring of actin and type II myosin. By contrast, plant cytokinesis starts in the centre of the division plane and progresses laterally. A transient membrane compartment, the cell plate, is formed <i>de novo</i> by fusion of Golgi-derived vesicles trafficking along the phragmoplast MTs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Heese, M., Mayer, U. &amp; Jurgens, G. Cytokinesis in flowering plants: cellular process and developmental integration. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol.  1, 486–491 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3376">80</a></sup>. Consistent with the unique mode of plant cytokinesis, <i>Arabidopsis</i> appears to lack genes for septins and type II myosin. Conversely, cell-plate formation requires a cytokinesis-specific syntaxin that has no close homologue in yeast and animals. Although syntaxin-mediated membrane fusion occurs in animal cytokinesis and cellularization, the vesicles are delivered to the base of the cleavage furrow. Thus, the plant-specific mechanism of cell division is linked to conserved eukaryotic cell-cycle machinery. </p><p>Two main conclusions are suggested by this comparative analysis. First, <i> Arabidopsis</i> and eukaryotic cells have common features related to intracellular activities, such as vesicle trafficking, cytoskeleton and cell cycle. Second, evolutionarily divergent features, such as organization of the cytoskeleton and cytokinesis, appear to relate to the plant cell wall.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Development"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Development</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><p>The regulation of development in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, as in animals, involves cell–cell communication, hierarchies of transcription factors, and the regulation of chromatin state; however, there is no reason to suppose that the complex multicellular states of plant and animal development have evolved by elaborating the same general processes during the 1.6 billion years since the last common unicellular ancestor of plants and animals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Meyerowitz, E. M. Plants, animals, and the logic of development. Trends Genet.  15, M65–M68 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3400">81</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Wang, D. Y. C. et al. Divergence time estimates for the early history of animal phyla and the origin of plants, animals and fungi. Proc. R. Soc. Lond. B Bio. 266, 63–171 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3403">82</a></sup>. Our genome analyses reflect the long, independent evolution of many processes contributing to development in the two kingdoms.</p><p>Plants and animals have converged on similar processes of pattern formation, but have used and expanded different transcription factor families as key causal regulators. For example, segmentation in insects and differentiation along the anterior–posterior and limb axes in mammals both involve the spatially specific activation of a series of homeobox gene family members. The pattern of activation is causal in the later differentiation of body and limb axis regions. In plants the pattern of floral whorls (sepals, petals, stamens, carpels) is also established by the spatially specific activation of members of a family of transcription factors, but in this instance the family is the MADS box family. Plants also have homeobox genes and animals have MADS box genes, implying that each lineage invented separately its mechanism of spatial pattern formation, while converging on actions and interactions of transcription factors as the mechanism. Other examples show even greater divergence of plant and animal developmental control. Examples are the AP2/EREBP and NAC families of transcription factors, which have important roles in flower and meristem development; both families are so far found only in plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> Table 6).</p><p>A similar story can be told for cell–cell communication. Plants do not seem to have receptor tyrosine kinases, but the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome has at least 340 genes for receptor Ser/Thr kinases, belonging to many different families, defined by their putative extracellular domains (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1"> Supplementary Information</a> Table 7). Several families have members with known functions in cell–cell communication, such as the CLV1 receptor involved in meristem cell signalling, the S-glycoprotein homologues involved in signalling from pollen to stigma in self-incompatible <i>Brassica</i> species, and the BRI1 receptor necessary for brassinosteroid signalling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Torii, K. Receptor kinase activation and signal transduction in plants: an emerging picture. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 3, 362– 367 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3425">83</a></sup>. Animals also have receptor Ser/Thr kinases, such as the transforming growth factor-β (TGF-β) receptors, but these act through SMAD proteins that are absent from <i>Arabidopsis</i>. The leucine-rich repeat (LRR) family of <i>Arabidopsis</i> receptor kinases shares its extracellular domain with many animal and fungal proteins that do not have associated kinase domains, and there are at least 122 <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes that code for LRR proteins without a kinase domain. Other <i>Arabidopsis</i> receptor kinase families have extracellular domains that are unfamiliar in animals. Thus, evolution is modular, and the plant and animal lineages have expanded different families of receptor kinases for a similar set of developmental processes.</p><p>Several <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes of developmental importance appear to be derived from a cyanobacteria-like genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information </a> Table 2), with no close relationship to any animal or fungal protein. One salient example is the family of ethylene receptors; another gene family of apparent chloroplast origin is the phytochromes—light receptors involved in many developmental decisions (see below). Whereas the land plant phytochromes show clear homology to the cyanobacterial light receptors, which are typical prokaryotic histidine kinases, the plant phytochromes are histidine kinase paralogues with Ser/Thr specificity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Yeh, K. C. &amp; Lagarias, J. C. Eukaryotic phytochromes: Light-regulated serine/threonine protein kinases with histidine kinase ancestry.  Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 95, 13976– 13981 (1998)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3451">84</a></sup>. Similarly to the ethylene receptors, the proteins that act downstream of plant phytochrome signalling are not found in cyanobacteria, and thus it appears that a bacterial light receptor entered the plant genome through horizontal transfer, altered its enzymatic activity, and became linked to a eukaryotic signal transduction pathway. This infusion of genes from a cyanobacterial endosymbiont shows that plants have a richer heritage of ancestral genes than animals, and unique developmental processes that derive from horizontal gene transfer.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Signal transduction "><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Signal transduction </h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><p>Being generally sessile organisms, plants have to respond to local environmental conditions by changing their physiology or redirecting their growth. Signals from the environment include light and pathogen attack, temperature, water, nutrients, touch and gravity. In addition to local cellular responses, some stimuli are communicated across the plant body, with plant hormones and peptides acting as secondary messengers. Some hormones, such as auxin, are taken up into the cell, whereas others, such as ethylene and brassinosteroids, and the peptide CLV3, act as ligands for receptor kinases on the plasma membrane. No matter where the signal is perceived by the cell, it is transduced to the nucleus, resulting in altered patterns of gene expression.</p><p>Comparative genome analysis between <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>C. elegans</i> and <i>Drosophila</i> supports the idea that plants have evolved their own pathways of signal transduction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="McCarty, D. R. &amp; Chory, J. Conservation and innovation in plant signaling pathways. Cell 103, 201–211 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3475">85</a></sup>. None of the components of the widely adopted signalling pathways found in vertebrates, flies or worms, such as Wingless/Wnt, Hedgehog, Notch/lin12, JAK/STAT, TGF-β/SMADs, receptor tyrosine kinase/Ras or the nuclear steroid hormone receptors, is found in <i> Arabidopsis</i>. By contrast, brassinosteroids are ligands of the BRI1 Ser/Thr kinase, a member of the largest recognizable class of transmembrane sensors encoded by 340&nbsp;receptor-like kinase (RLK) genes in the <i>Arabidopsis </i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> Table 7). With a few notable exceptions, such as CLV1, the types of ligands sensed by RLKs are completely unknown, providing an enormous future challenge for plant biologists. G-protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs)/ seven-transmembrane proteins are an abundant class of proteins in mammalian genomes, instrumental in signal transduction. INTERPRO detected 27 GPCR-related domains in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/35048692#MOESM1"> Supplementary Information</a> Table 1), although there is no direct experimental evidence for these. <i>Arabidopsis</i> contains a family of 18 seven-transmembrane proteins of the mildew resistance (MlO) class, several of which are involved in defence responses. Notably, only single Gα (GPA1) and Gβ (AGB1) subunits are found in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, both previously known<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Weiss, C. A., Garnaat, C., Mukai, K., Hu, Y. &amp; Ma, H. Molecular cloning of cDNAs from maize and Arabidopsis encoding a G protein beta subunit. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 91, 9554–9558 (1994)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3502">86</a></sup>. </p><p>Although cyclic GMP has been proposed to be involved in signal transduction in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Bowler, C.  et al. Cyclic GMP and calcium mediate phytochrome phototransduction. Cell 77, 73–81 (1994)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3511">87</a></sup>, a protein containing a guanylate cyclase domain was not identified in our analyses. Nevertheless, cyclic nucleotide-binding domains were detected in various proteins, indicating that cNMPs may have a role in plant signal transduction. Thus, although cNMP-binding domains appear to have been conserved during evolution, cNMP synthesis in <i>Arabidopsis </i> may have evolved independently.</p><p>We were unable to identify a protein with significant similarity to known Gγ subunits, but recent biochemical studies suggest that a protein with this functional capacity is likely to be present in plant cells (H. Ma, personal communication). Therefore, there is potential for the formation of only a single heterotrimeric G-protein complex; however, its functional interaction with any of the potential GPCR-related proteins remains to be determined. </p><p>Modules of cellular signal pathways from bacteria and animals have been combined and new cascades have been innovated in plants. A pertinent example is the response to the gaseous plant hormone ethylene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Stepanova, A. &amp; Ecker, J. R. Ethylene signaling: from mutants to molecules. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 3, 353–360 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3525">88</a></sup>. Ethylene is perceived and its signal transmitted by a family of receptors related to bacterial-type two-component histidine kinases (HKs). In bacteria, yeast and plants, these proteins sense many extracellular signals and function in a His-to-Asp phosphorelay network<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Urao, T., Yamaguchi-Shinozaki, K. &amp; Shinozaki, K. Two-component systems in plant signal transduction. Trends Plant Sci. 5, 67– 74 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3529">89</a></sup>. In turn, these proteins physically interact with the genetically downstream protein CTR1, a Raf/MAPKKK-related kinase, revealing the juxtaposition of bacterial-type two-component receptors and animal-type MAP kinase cascades. Unlike animals, however, <i>Arabidopsis </i> does not seem to have a Ras protein to activate the MAP kinase cascade. MAP kinases are found in abundance in <i>Arabidopsis</i>: we identified <span class="stix">∼</span>20, a higher number than in any other eukaryote. As potentially counteracting components, we found <span class="stix">∼</span>70 putative PP2C protein phosphatases. Although this group is largely uncharacterized functionally, several members are related to ABI1/ABI2, key negative regulators in the signalling pathway for the plant hormone abscisic acid. Additional components of the His-to-Asp phosphorelay system were also found in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, including authentic response regulators (ARRs), pseudoresponse regulators (PRRs) and phosphotransfer intermediate protein (HPt)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Makino, S.  et al. Genes encoding pseudo-response regulators: Insight into His-to-Asp phosphorelay and circadian rhythm in Arabidopsis thaliana.  Plant Cell Physiol. 41, 791–803 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3543">90</a></sup>. We found 11 HKs in the proteome (3 new), 16 RRs (2 new) and 8 PRRs (2 new). The biological roles of most ARRs, PRRs and HPts are largly unknown, but several have been found to&nbsp;have diverse functions in plants, including transcriptional activation in response to the plant hormone cytokinin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="D'Agostino, I. B. &amp; Kieber, J. J. Phosphorelay signal transduction: the emerging family of plant response regulators.  Trends Biol. Sci. 24, 452–456 (1999)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3547">91</a></sup>, and as components of the circadian clock<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Strayer, C.  et al. Cloning of the Arabidopsis clock gene TOC1, an autoregulatory response regulator homologue. Science 289, 768–771 (2000)." href="/articles/35048692#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d33224e3551">92</a></sup>.</p><p>Plants seem to have evolved unique signalling pathways by combining a conserved MAP kinase cascade module with new receptor types. In many cases, however, the ligands are unknown. Conversely, some known signalling molecules, such as auxin, are still in search of a receptor. Auxin signalling may represent yet another plant-specific mode of signalling, with protein degradation through the ubiquitin-proteasome pathway preceding altered gene expression.
